    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1534104558" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1534104558" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1534104558?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/3896914-the-overcoat">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1534104558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6583093475437570598"><br/>It is a simple tale, on the surface. Akaky Akakievich (literally "Harmless Son-of-Harmless," but which might sound like "Poopy Pooperson” to a child), an impoverished civil servant and scrivener, must maintain his respectability by possessing a decent overcoat. How he gains a new overcoat, loses that overcoat, and seeks to have the overcoat restored to him constitutes the whole of our story. <br/><br/>Dostoevsky has been quoted as saying, “We all come from under Gogol's <i>Overcoat</i>", and it is true that much</span>
  <span id="freeText6583093475437570598" style="display:none"><br />It is a simple tale, on the surface. Akaky Akakievich (literally "Harmless Son-of-Harmless," but which might sound like "Poopy Pooperson” to a child), an impoverished civil servant and scrivener, must maintain his respectability by possessing a decent overcoat. How he gains a new overcoat, loses that overcoat, and seeks to have the overcoat restored to him constitutes the whole of our story. <br /><br />Dostoevsky has been quoted as saying, “We all come from under Gogol's <i>Overcoat</i>", and it is true that much of Russian literature can be glimpsed in this single short story: it is a satire ranging from buffonery to social commentary, a realist work rooted in naturalistic detail that sometimes descends to the grotesque and the surreal, and yet remains compassionate, maintaining its sympathy for all of us humans and our tragic and ludicrous plight. Not bad for a story slightly more than twelve thousand words in length.<br /><br />Which brings us to the distinctive characteristic of Gogol: he is a literary conjurer, with an extraordinary ability to shift from tone to tone. <i>The Overcoat</i> begins in low comedy, making fun of its character's name, then describes his shabby living conditions until we begin to see the dead flies and smell the onions. Gogol ridicules his protagonist's rigidity and pomposity, but then—when some younger clerks make fun of him—Gogol shifts his tone again until we grow to regard Akaky with an abiding compassion. From there, Gogol sharpens his social satire, tempering it with a comedy touched with pathos, and ends—not in tragedy, as we suspect it might, but—in nightmare and the supernatural.<br /><br />We'll let Nabokov have the last word. “[W]ith Gogol this shifting is the very basis of his art... When, as in the immortal <i>The Overcoat</i>, he really let himself go and pottered on the brink of his private abyss, he became the greatest artist that Russia has yet produced.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="6583093475437570598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1915077226" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1915077226" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1915077226?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=russian-lit">russian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1915077226" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1824470556728678778">Recently I read The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri about two generations of an Indian immigrant family to the United States. The main theme of the novel was that the father Ashoke was reading The Overcoat on a train journey. The train derailed and this slim book saved his life. Indebted to the book, Ashoke decided to name his newborn son Nikhil but gave him the nickname Gogol, after the Russian writer whose works he adored. Lahiri even includes snippets of Gogol's life in her novel, but until now I h</span>
  <span id="freeText1824470556728678778" style="display:none">Recently I read The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri about two generations of an Indian immigrant family to the United States. The main theme of the novel was that the father Ashoke was reading The Overcoat on a train journey. The train derailed and this slim book saved his life. Indebted to the book, Ashoke decided to name his newborn son Nikhil but gave him the nickname Gogol, after the Russian writer whose works he adored. Lahiri even includes snippets of Gogol's life in her novel, but until now I had not read any of his work. It is in this regard, that I chose to read The Overcoat to get a feel for the story that had such a central place in the book. <br /><br />Nicolai Gogol lived a brief existence from 1809-1852, primarily in Petersburg, Russia, with a few sojourns to Rome. Gogol was a gifted writer who influenced Dostoevsky and Turgenev among others. Sent away to boarding school at the age of ten, Gogol lived most of his life away from his father, and did not have other family to speak of. As a result, he had compassion for the less fortunate of people, and this becomes evident in his famous short story. <br /><br />Gogol got the idea for The Overcoat at a party with friends when he heard of a civil servant who desires a new gun but could not afford one and saved money in order to do so. Thus, the idea for Akaky Akakyevich and his overcoat was born. Akakyevich survived on a salary of 450 rubles a year and could barely afford food or clothes. He was happy at his job as a copier and had no desire to advance in his profession or to marry. Living in a tiny apartment in the poor side of town, Akakyevich brought home extra work and saved rubles in a tin in order to have the means to afford repairs to his clothing. <br /><br />One year, his Overcoat was worn beyond repair, and Akaky Akakyevich saved rubles in his tin for a new coat. He convinced his tailor Petrovich in a drunken moment to sew him a new one for only 80 rubles and by doing so was able to survive for another winter. Despite his joy in receiving the new coat, Akakyevich's moment is short lived; however, because others coveted his coat and stole it from him. This fleeting moment sets up a memorable denouement that appears to be out of a folk tale. <br /><br />I thought The Overcoat was straight forward and accessible to read. I enjoyed the message that Gogol sent to his readers to have compassion on others regardless of one's station in life, and was especially moved when Akakyevich's colleagues took up collection for a new Overcoat for him. This is the second short story I have read by a Russian master this year, and I am glad to see that the stories are folktales in nature and easy to read. I hope stories as The Overcoat lead me to read some of the longer, classic Russian literature, and am glad that Jhumpa Lahiri's novel inspired me to read the works of Nicolai Gogol.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1824470556728678778" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1462491014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1462491014" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1462491014?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=wallowing-in-misery-and-angst">wallowing-in-misery-and-angst</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=the-shorts">the-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=things-go-bump-in-the-night">things-go-bump-in-the-night</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=east-europe-russia">east-europe-russia</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1462491014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7528827237409115854">2020 update: I'm bumping this up to all 5 stars on reread. This Russian tale of an introverted man and his trials relating to an expensive (for him) overcoat really hit me on second read. The characterization is so in-depth for a shorter work, especially as it relates to Akaky, the main character, his tailor, and a small-minded bureaucrat. There's also some really interesting symbolism relating to his overcoat and how it affects both Akaky and the people around him. Recommended!<br/><br/>The English trans</span>
  <span id="freeText7528827237409115854" style="display:none">2020 update: I'm bumping this up to all 5 stars on reread. This Russian tale of an introverted man and his trials relating to an expensive (for him) overcoat really hit me on second read. The characterization is so in-depth for a shorter work, especially as it relates to Akaky, the main character, his tailor, and a small-minded bureaucrat. There's also some really interesting symbolism relating to his overcoat and how it affects both Akaky and the people around him. Recommended!<br /><br />The English translation on Project Gutenberg (linked at the end of this review) from the original Russian is excellent, except that it still irks me that it's called "the cloak" rather than the overcoat. (It has sleeves; it's an overcoat)<br /><br />Original review: In my preparation for reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/485894.The_Metamorphosis" title="The Metamorphosis by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Metamorphosis</a>, I did some background reading of critical analyses, including <a href="http://www.kafka.org/index.php?id=191,209,0,0,1,0" rel="nofollow noopener">this one</a> by Vladimir Nabokov (thanks to Cecily for the link!), where he does a fantastic dissection (heh) of <i>The Metamorphosis</i> but also talks about Gogol's "The Carrick" (aka "The Cloak" or "The Overcoat") and tosses off wonderful ideas like this:<blockquote>"The beauty of Kafka's and Gogol's private nightmares is that their central human characters belong to the same private fantastic world as the inhuman characters around them, but the central one tries to get out of that world, to cast off the mask, to transcend the cloak or the carapace."</blockquote>And then there's this haunting quote, attributed to Fyodor Dostoevsky: "We all come out from Gogol's 'Overcoat'." (ETA: Even months later, every time I think about this story, that quote comes to mind.) So off I went to read Nicolai Gogol's short story.<br /><br />Akaky Akakievich is an absurd, pathetic figure of a man. His name would translate as something very nondescript like "John Johnson," except you also have this deliberate allusion to "kaka" (or caca = feces) in his name; one review site suggested you think of him as Poopy McPooperson. He is a "titular councillor" (read: minor official) who in fact does nothing except act as a human photocopier, all day, every day, for very low pay. He even takes his copy work home with him in the evenings. His only joy in life is derived from his copy work. Even being asked to make the most minor changes to the original version throws him into a tizzy. His co-workers make fun of him, but other than a pitiful protest of "Leave me alone! Why do you insult me?," he quietly carries on.<br /><br />Until one day, when he realizes that his overcoat has become so threadbare that it won't keep off the cold St. Petersburg winter. After a few skirmishes with his tailor about whether the old coat can be patched up or not, he caves and agrees to save up money for a new coat, which will cost like 20% of his annual wages. Gradually Akaky gets more and more excited about his new coat. And when he finally gets the finished overcoat -- lined with cat fur because marten fur is too expensive (sorry to my feline-loving friends!) -- it causes a sensation in his workplace.<br /><br />Of course, this being 19th century Russian literature, you know it's going to go south for poor Akaky. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Akaky's coat gets stolen the first day, when he goes out at night to a party his co-workers throw to celebrate his new coat. He fruitlessly tries to get the police and some government guy to help him, gets totally shot down, walks home in the freezing cold, gets sick and dies. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> But the surprise for me was the ending. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The ghost of Akaky Akakievich starts haunting St. Petersburg, pulling coats off of pedestrians. And is that a tougher, more masculine Akaky ghost at the very end, or a different ghost? I'm not quite certain but I'm inclined to think it's Akaky. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I'm still a little bemused by the unexpected turn from existentialist dark humor to gothic at the end. It didn't quite feel integral to me, Nabokov's inspired praises notwithstanding. So, four stars from me, even though I actually though it was an amusing ending and I liked seeing a certain character get his comeuppance.<br /><br />Free online several places, including <a href="http://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/13437" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> at Project Gutenberg in a collection of Russian short stories. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7528827237409115854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_702236617" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/702236617" />
    <a title="Florencia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia"><img alt="Florencia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601866797p2/21575078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/702236617?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florencia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florencia" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia">Florencia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">The entire human race</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=russian">russian</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer702236617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4895465734429397108">My first contact with Gogol, and certainly not my last. <br/>This little book tells the story of Akakiy Akakievitch, a certain official in a certain department where nobody showed him any sign of respect. He was laughed at by his co-workers. That must be one of the worst thing that may happen to any human being: realizing that high school did not end (for a lot of people, it wasn't all flowers and rainbows). All the bullying, the bad jokes, the embarrassing moments that make you gently ask the ground</span>
  <span id="freeText4895465734429397108" style="display:none">My first contact with Gogol, and certainly not my last. <br />This little book tells the story of Akakiy Akakievitch, a certain official in a certain department where nobody showed him any sign of respect. He was laughed at by his co-workers. That must be one of the worst thing that may happen to any human being: realizing that high school did not end (for a lot of people, it wasn't all flowers and rainbows). All the bullying, the bad jokes, the embarrassing moments that make you gently ask the ground to eat you alive, the psychological violence you cannot get rid of, all that, now... at your workplace? You have to love the irony. <br /><br /><i>The Overcoat</i> is, well, a story about an overcoat. It seems to have more importance than Akakiy himself, the responsible guy with the unfortunate name. That is another thing... mothers, what the hell are you thinking when you give your children ridiculous names? Please, spare them a lot of trouble and save yourselves a lot of money in psychologists and start naming your kids properly. I don't know why they don't change their own name into some fruit, weird magicians, comic superheroes, cars, cardinal points or whatever they seem to love. Especially you, celebrity people who don't know I exist and won't read this in your entire life! <br />Okay. Rant officially over. (If you search for "Akakiy Akakievitch", you will understand. I had to do that because I wanted to know why the author spent several lines explaining how he got his name and yeah, I don't speak Russian.)<br /><br />As I was saying, this book is about <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[a man who was constantly humiliated at work and his ruined overcoat, which he wanted to repair because of the cold, cold winter and the bad, bad jokes. So he decided to buy a new one and after living under a tight budget, he managed to do so. And suddenly, he was a respectable man. That laughable poor devil who always endured those vicious jokes and never replied to anyone, was now a significant part of his department; of society, even. His brand new overcoat gave him confidence, some self esteem. People at his department even organized a party in his honor. Actually, in the overcoat's honor but still, it was a big deal. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> And then something happened... <br /><br />I loved this story. I found some honest and beautiful lines...<br /><blockquote>...and many a time afterwards, in the course of his life, shuddered at seeing how much inhumanity there is in man, how much savage coarseness is concealed beneath delicate, refined worldliness, and even, O God! in that man whom the world acknowledges as honorable and noble.</blockquote><br />...that reflect society back then. And now. Everything seems to change but the most remarkable aspects don't change <i>that</i> much. That is one of the reasons I love literature. Books written hundreds of years ago reflect situations, attitudes, emotions, ways of thinking that we see nowadays. Feelings towards routine and overbearing bureaucracy, discrimination, injustice, exploitation, alienation are the same two centuries ago and now. Not all writers have what it takes to explore these universal emotions and write something that you can immediately relate to. But Gogol seems to be one of them. Apparently, he had that keen eye meant to observe individuals and humanity as a whole, and was able to write about it in such a beautiful manner (I could totally see my previous boss in some pages).<br /> <br />Gogol's influence on Russian literature is unquestionable. Dostoyevsky, Bulgakov. It also appears in Kafka's work, so my favorite authors are kind of connected here. <br /><i>The Overcoat</i> is a short story that contains too much. Do not miss it.<br /><br /><br /><br />Sep 7, 2013<br />* Also on <a href="https://nonbookreviews.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4895465734429397108" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2721256348" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2721256348" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2721256348?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/24381671-the-overcoat">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=short-story-novella">short-story-novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=russian-lit">russian-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2721256348" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6977835830352293473"><i> The Overcoat </i> tells the story of life and death of one Akaky Akakievich, a government official in a certain department. The first part of the story introduces us to the personality of Akaky and his poor living conditions. The job though satisfying doesn't earn enough to keep him well clothed and bred. He is extremely reserved and becomes a constant subject of ridicule. Gogol plants Akaky well in the reader’s hearts in this first part arousing their compassion. Akaky, after much labour and suffe</span>
  <span id="freeText6977835830352293473" style="display:none"><i> The Overcoat </i> tells the story of life and death of one Akaky Akakievich, a government official in a certain department. The first part of the story introduces us to the personality of Akaky and his poor living conditions. The job though satisfying doesn't earn enough to keep him well clothed and bred. He is extremely reserved and becomes a constant subject of ridicule. Gogol plants Akaky well in the reader’s hearts in this first part arousing their compassion. Akaky, after much labour and suffering, becomes a proud owner of an overcoat. And here ends the first part of the story. <br /><br />In the second part of the story, the reader learns the misfortune of Akaky. He is robbed of his overcoat; his efforts at recovering his lost property are rendered futile. His disappointment over his loss and the exposure to ill weather in its absence sees him to an early grave. But here the story gets better, for Akaky comes back from the dead to seek justice and takes revenge from those who had failed to help him!<br />	<br />This little story tells many things: It exposes the poverty-stricken lives of middle class working people; it shows the uncompassionate and bullying nature of the humans; it brings to light the inefficiency and unjust and unsatisfactory conduct of government bureaucrats of Russia under the Imperial regime. Akaky’s ghost haunting the officials is kind of a hint that someday the tolerance for such governance might end in a catastrophe (as was seen years later).<br /><br />Gogol is said to be a pioneer in realistic writing. His writing as is portrayed in <i> The Overcoat </i> is touched on real characters and real themes that concern human society. Dostoevsky once said that “We all come from Gogol’s Overcoat” and this is a very good indication that how influential Gogol’s work had been on Russian literature. <br /><br />There is an easy grace in his writing which makes it undemanding to read. It is one of the best attributes of his writing. His direct and at times sardonic writing is quite appealing. It is not right to draw comparisons between the literary masters especially from different literary traditions, but so far the writing is concerned, I couldn’t help comparing his writing with that of Charles Dickens and Oscar Wild.<br /><br />Overall, I enjoyed this short work. Personally I cannot place Gogol in the same light with Tolstoy and Dostoevsky, but I do like his style.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6977835830352293473" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1925510657" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1925510657" />
    <a title="Iris P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2641948-iris-p"><img alt="Iris P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483406411p2/2641948.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1925510657?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iris P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iris P" href="/user/show/2641948-iris-p">Iris P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25912359-the-overcoat-annotated">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers interested in short stories, classical literature</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=russian-lit">russian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=classic-literature">classic-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1925510657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10861875766233151857"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/537094.The_Overcoat' title='The Overcoat by Nikolai Gogol' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Overcoat</a><br/><br/>★★★★ 4 Engaging Stars!<br/><br/><br/>I first heard of this story while reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3670.Jhumpa_Lahiri' title='Jhumpa Lahiri' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jhumpa Lahiri</a>'s 2003 fantastic novel <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11214027.The_Namesake' title='The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Namesake</a>. Since then, I've been curious to read it and I finally had a chance to do it. (If you Google "The Namesake and The Overcoat", you'll find plenty of posts analyzing the connection between the two).<br/><br/><i>The Overcoat </i>follows the life and death of Akaky Akakievich, a middle-aged man, that works as a government clerk in St. Petersburg. Akaky, whose annual salary of 400 rubles bare</span>
  <span id="freeText10861875766233151857" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/537094.The_Overcoat" title="The Overcoat by Nikolai Gogol" rel="nofollow noopener">The Overcoat</a><br><br>★★★★ 4 Engaging Stars!<br><br><br>I first heard of this story while reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3670.Jhumpa_Lahiri" title="Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">Jhumpa Lahiri</a>'s 2003 fantastic novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11214027.The_Namesake" title="The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">The Namesake</a>. Since then, I've been curious to read it and I finally had a chance to do it. (If you Google "The Namesake and The Overcoat", you'll find plenty of posts analyzing the connection between the two).<br><br><i>The Overcoat </i>follows the life and death of Akaky Akakievich, a middle-aged man, that works as a government clerk in St. Petersburg. Akaky, whose annual salary of 400 rubles barely allows him to survive, is deeply passionate about his job. He's a recluse, neglects his appearance and never pays attention to what is happening around him. Because of his odd personality and isolated lifestyle, his coworkers constantly make fun of him, but Akaky rarely lets this affect his performance at work.<br><br>One day after he notices that his back is hurting, Akaky soon determines that the problem is that his overcoat is so worn out that is not protecting him from the icy winds that blow through the city. He decides to take the item to his tailor, Petrovitch, to see if he can mend it. After some back and forth between the two, Petrovitch tells Akaky that there's no way to patch the coat and that he'll have to buy a new one. <br><br><a href="http://s1341.photobucket.com/user/irisper01/media/The_Overcoat_by_Nikolai_Gogol_zpsbxcyrhbx.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495752163i/22853373._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo The_Overcoat_by_Nikolai_Gogol_zpsbxcyrhbx.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></a><br><b><i>A stamp depicting "The Overcoat", from the souvenir sheet of Russia devoted to the 200th birth anniversary of Nikolai Gogol, 2009 (Via Wikipedia)</i></b><br><br>Initially, Akaky is taken aback when Petrovitch estimates that a new overcoat will cost around 150 rubles, more than a third of his annual salary. But eventually, he resigns himself to the idea and, paradoxically, discovers that the quest to put aside the money has actually brought a new sense of purpose to his otherwise dull existence. After receiving a higher than expected bonus, along with some money he's been saving, Akaky is finally ready to purchase his new piece of garment. <br><br>The day he receives the coat is the happiest of Akaky's life. Sadly, his elation is short-lived as a sudden turn of events ends with the loss of his coat, a devastating illness and ultimately, his own demise. Soon after he dies, Akaky returns as a ghost to haunt those that were cruel to him, in particular, a superintendent that refused to help him when the overcoat was reported lost. <br><br>For a story published in 1842, I found the language of <i>The Overcoat</i> surprisingly approachable. Gogol's not so veiled criticism of the Russian political establishment as well as his sardonic depiction of government bureaucrats was particularly delightful.<br><br>An engaging, well-written story that can be read in less than an hour. Recommended for those that enjoy classic literature and short stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10861875766233151857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2513209142" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2513209142" />

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1265602911" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer18169803138360116430"><b>THE OVERCOAT is a classic Russian satire first published in 1842. It is an atmospheric short story packed with substance and emotion.</b><p><b>THE OVERCOAT</b> belongs to AA, an extremely poor man with an extremely undemanding, meagerly-paying government job, but he diligently completes his work day and night. He is criticized for his apparel and lacks social acceptance.</p><p><b>THE OVERCOAT</b> is old...torn...threadbare...can no longer be mended. AA is sad...devastated, he lacks rubles for a new one. He must curtail ord</p></span>
  <span id="freeText18169803138360116430" style="display:none"><b>THE OVERCOAT is a classic Russian satire first published in 1842. It is an atmospheric short story packed with substance and emotion.</b><p><b>THE OVERCOAT</b> belongs to AA, an extremely poor man with an extremely undemanding, meagerly-paying government job, but he diligently completes his work day and night. He is criticized for his apparel and lacks social acceptance.</p><p><b>THE OVERCOAT</b> is old...torn...threadbare...can no longer be mended. AA is sad...devastated, he lacks rubles for a new one. He must curtail ordinary expenses for at least a year...no tea...no candles...he must walk on his toes to save the heels of his shoes and suffer hunger in the darkness of the evenings.</p><p><b>THE</b> <i>(new)</i> <b>OVERCOAT</b> brings happiness...yay!...a party to celebrate...social acceptance...and then...<b>oh no!......it can't be!</b></p><p>The story continues in a direction I truly <b>did not</b> expect, and if you want to be surprised, <b>go in cold turkey. There are some wonderful reviews out there, but they give away the outcome.</b></p><p>Enjoyed my first Nikolai Gogol read, and look forward to more!</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="18169803138360116430" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1801234449" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1801234449" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1801234449?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781409931294" href="/book/show/6249574-the-overcoat">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=russia-ukraine">russia-ukraine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1801234449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4721213518010164143">One of the great short-stories from one of the most influential writers of the 19th century. This is only brief in length but comes across as very perceptive of the human condition. Told with a splash of subtle humour and with themes of vanity, self-delusion, and poverty, Gogol's dizzying tale looks at one Akaky Akakiyevich, a St Petersburg office clerk who buys and then loses a new cloak with a cat-fur collar. Whilst wearing it he is transformed into a different person, one of sociable acceptan</span>
  <span id="freeText4721213518010164143" style="display:none">One of the great short-stories from one of the most influential writers of the 19th century. This is only brief in length but comes across as very perceptive of the human condition. Told with a splash of subtle humour and with themes of vanity, self-delusion, and poverty, Gogol's dizzying tale looks at one Akaky Akakiyevich, a St Petersburg office clerk who buys and then loses a new cloak with a cat-fur collar. Whilst wearing it he is transformed into a different person, one of sociable acceptance. A highlight, and one of the best moments, is when the pompous boss begins to feel guilty about his treatment of poor Akaky, so goes off to console himself with his mistress. Gogol also, in such a short space of time time shows Akaky to be inscrutable and ethereal, yet pitiably real. One of hardest things in the short-story format is getting the characters across as believable and bringing them to life in the quickest time possible, but, without the need to rush things. Gogol pulls it off, almost as good as the master of the Russian short-story Anton Chekhov. A light-hearted read that is surreal, funny, and tinged with tragedy. Still can't quite believe how old it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4721213518010164143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16842444846995158158" style="display:none">It been a long time since I saw the movie 'The Namesake'. It's a beautiful movie starring Irfan Khan and Tabu.       <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<div id="review_3490241562" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3490241562" />
    <a title="Théodore " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/98232817-th-odore"><img alt="Théodore " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595517509p2/98232817.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3490241562?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Théodore " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Théodore " href="/user/show/98232817-th-odore">Théodore </a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9782290344491" href="/book/show/1861978.Le_manteau_">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3490241562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17255952152168565062">Absurd. Classic. Fantastic. <br/>In brief , Gogol.<br/><br/>I had my first contact with Gogol a long time ago, during school, and that story " The Nose " - remained imprinted in my mind as a kind of "Invisible Man" of Wells, remembering only the fantastic of writing. <br/> Now, reading " The Mantle", after so many years, I must admit that the fantastic is not the element that jumped into my eyes. <br/> The universe created by Gogol is an absurd one, and the movement and action are, in fact, illusory, the characters them</span>
  <span id="freeText17255952152168565062" style="display:none">Absurd. Classic. Fantastic. <br />In brief , Gogol.<br /><br />I had my first contact with Gogol a long time ago, during school, and that story " The Nose " - remained imprinted in my mind as a kind of "Invisible Man" of Wells, remembering only the fantastic of writing. <br /> Now, reading " The Mantle", after so many years, I must admit that the fantastic is not the element that jumped into my eyes. <br /> The universe created by Gogol is an absurd one, and the movement and action are, in fact, illusory, the characters themselves being just simulacra. Beyond the level of the absurd, not even a space of allegory is revealed. <br /> In " The Mantle", the author points out that human decay is so profound, that society has become quite unable to perceive the wrong mechanisms, and the situations in which a character still manages to capture the true face of reality - end in a tragic note. <br /> Heroes, - or rather anti heroes - can no longer bear to live in the same way as before, despair pushing them to the gesture of suicide. The scale of decay that the author draws, comprises various stages. If in " The Nose" - the element of the unpredictable appears from the beginning, in " The Mantle" it intervenes only at the end, through the ghost of the former office clerk, who haunted the streets of Petersburg, stealing mantles from people's shoulders...<br /> Pettiness is subjected to a thorough analysis in this short story of only 76 pages, and the world, here, is one of masks ( classic masks, not like nowadays...) , of the characters acting by virtue of inertia. <br /> At the textual level, the rendered world - apparently keeping recognizable coordinates - no longer has much in common with the reality to which readers refer. <br /> Gogol's fantasy does not establish a boundary between the real and the fictional, but, on the contrary - brings into the "normal" world elements that disturb the established order, making reality slip into an absurdity drawn by a series of coordinates, with which we are accustomed only in the literature of the twentieth century.<br /> Personally, I see Gogol somewhere between Zamiatin - the promoter of the "We " -" The Brave New World " cicle - and Dostoïevski, with a keen sense of the human fantastic, moreover.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17255952152168565062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1956756724485911980" style="display:none">4.5 stars. <div id="review_200864437" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/200864437" />
    <a title="Gloria Mundi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4600483-gloria-mundi"><img alt="Gloria Mundi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312494055p2/4600483.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/200864437?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gloria Mundi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gloria Mundi" href="/user/show/4600483-gloria-mundi">Gloria Mundi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4600483-gloria-mundi?shelf=ghosties">ghosties</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4600483-gloria-mundi?shelf=russian">russian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4600483-gloria-mundi?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4600483-gloria-mundi?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer200864437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer455949127951608390">I must have read this for school as a kid. I have definitely read it a very long time ago and I can't imagine I would have done so off my own back. <br/><br/>Anyway. It is a fantastic piece of work, widely acknowledged to be one of the best Russian short stories ever written, if not The Best. I would highly recommend it whether you are already a lover of Russian literature or are just starting to explore it. The idea is simple enough, that every person, no matter how unattractive and inconsequential, dese</span>
  <span id="freeText455949127951608390" style="display:none">I must have read this for school as a kid. I have definitely read it a very long time ago and I can't imagine I would have done so off my own back. <br /><br />Anyway. It is a fantastic piece of work, widely acknowledged to be one of the best Russian short stories ever written, if not The Best. I would highly recommend it whether you are already a lover of Russian literature or are just starting to explore it. The idea is simple enough, that every person, no matter how unattractive and inconsequential, deserves some compassion and understanding. Yet there is so much in the measly 50 odd pages of this story. Comedy, tragedy, social satire and even a ghost story. It is one of the very very few books which has managed to make me laugh and cry. The influence that it had on later writers such as Dostoyevsky (who famously stated that "We all come out of Gogol's Overcoat"), Turgenev, Tolstoy, Kafka and many others is undeniable. <br /><br />It is a story about a "small person", a poor civil cervant who does not have any outstanding abilities, is not clever or ambitious, who is made fun of by his co-workers, but who is, essentially, harmless and who enjoys his mindless repetitive work. And it is a story about the stark impersonal casual cruelty with which the world treats such a person, about "...how much inhumanity there is in man, how much savage coarseness is concealed beneath refined, cultured, worldly civility...".<br /><br />The story is available free online, e.g. at Project Gutenberg or here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.englishlibrary.org/story_gogol.html">http://www.englishlibrary.org/story_g...</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="455949127951608390" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3252246288" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3252246288" />
    <a title="Ali Karimnejad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/93895577-ali-karimnejad"><img alt="Ali Karimnejad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591439739p2/93895577.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3252246288?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ali Karimnejad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ali Karimnejad" href="/user/show/93895577-ali-karimnejad">Ali Karimnejad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/52781808">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3252246288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13831909400442137717">A fantastic tragic comedy. Gogol masterfully begins his story with his humorous style only to end it with such a bitterness. He is actually criticizing his society sarcastically and harshly about what happens to Akakievich. All the humiliations and teasing Akakievich receives from his colleagues. All the absurdness of his behavior. And Gogol points his finger to his society. The irony is that he does this in a way which causes the reader laugh at Akakievich too, but it does not take very long. <br/><br/>T</span>
  <span id="freeText13831909400442137717" style="display:none">A fantastic tragic comedy. Gogol masterfully begins his story with his humorous style only to end it with such a bitterness. He is actually criticizing his society sarcastically and harshly about what happens to Akakievich. All the humiliations and teasing Akakievich receives from his colleagues. All the absurdness of his behavior. And Gogol points his finger to his society. The irony is that he does this in a way which causes the reader laugh at Akakievich too, but it does not take very long. <br /><br />The “overcoat” is a metaphor. Metaphor of something which we may say “social class or social stratification”. Akakievich tries to change his “overcoat” but a hidden hand prevents him. He tries his best; he succeeds but at last a ghost-like thief kicks him in the butt to where he has come from.<br /><br /> Very impressing short story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13831909400442137717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3611197957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15808508852594703083">Here is a novel as we like them: an in-depth character wherein a few pages we identify the personality, a framework that holds from start to finish, a turnaround and a clear and clean end.<br/>Here we find a poor copyist, who only has on his back a simple worn-out hood with holes ... His dream: to be able to afford a coat. He saves every ruble until he can afford it, finally !!<br/>Yes but here it is, a few days after his previous purchases, he gets robbed, and everything drops ... until it dies.<br/>And there</span>
  <span id="freeText15808508852594703083" style="display:none">Here is a novel as we like them: an in-depth character wherein a few pages we identify the personality, a framework that holds from start to finish, a turnaround and a clear and clean end.<br />Here we find a poor copyist, who only has on his back a simple worn-out hood with holes ... His dream: to be able to afford a coat. He saves every ruble until he can afford it, finally !!<br />Yes but here it is, a few days after his previous purchases, he gets robbed, and everything drops ... until it dies.<br />And there Gogol takes the big step towards mysticism, by making his character return as a ghost-like a masked avenger who strips the inhabitants of their coats. He will not find rest until he has obtained the one that will have brought him death!</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1262746283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16192128896537326431">This is one of those iconic stories that is sure to leave an impressiion. It has scores of interpretations and almost everyone can find something in the story that resonates with them. It has a humorous slant to it, but it is also sad in it's depiction of the meaninglessness of life. If you do a search for the best short stories, you will find The Overcoat (The Cloak) right at or near the top. It's a well spent hour of reading.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16487316317787978093">My first story by Nikolai Gogol is definitely not my last. This quirky little ghost story is more humorous than scary in that it pokes fun of government bureaucrats in a manner not unlike that seen in several Dickens stories. </span>

<div id="review_135559246" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/135559246" />
    <a title="Amalie " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4164661-amalie"><img alt="Amalie " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600148509p2/4164661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/135559246?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amalie " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amalie " href="/user/show/4164661-amalie">Amalie </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4164661-amalie?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4164661-amalie?shelf=russian">russian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer135559246" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3517839240396013797">Dostoyevsky's quoted: "We all come out from Gogol's 'Overcoat'." Many critics introduce this as the greatest story in the Russian language and a key work in the evolution of Russian literature toward realism. Personally I love Russian short stories than novels or plays, I've found they are only short in their length and stretched in every other aspect. <br/><br/>The work has been interpreted variously as a story of social injustice, as tale of urban alienation and human isolation, as a love story with the</span>
  <span id="freeText3517839240396013797" style="display:none">Dostoyevsky's quoted: "We all come out from Gogol's 'Overcoat'." Many critics introduce this as the greatest story in the Russian language and a key work in the evolution of Russian literature toward realism. Personally I love Russian short stories than novels or plays, I've found they are only short in their length and stretched in every other aspect. <br /><br />The work has been interpreted variously as a story of social injustice, as tale of urban alienation and human isolation, as a love story with the coat serving as a metaphor for the love interest. <br /><br />It tells the story of an poor government clerk who lives a solitary life, when he realizes that his winter overcoat has become worn out he takes it to the tailor who tells him it cannot be mended. Then he undergoes extreme deprivation in order to save money for a new overcoat. In the process, the coat begins to take the central role in his life and he begins to view the garment as the key to his future happiness. I cannot come to a single interpretation it's more a mixed one, but I'm glad I read it, and so happy I enjoyed it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3517839240396013797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2612144905" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2612144905" />
    <a title="Jonfaith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith"><img alt="Jonfaith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534772484p2/2327886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2612144905?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonfaith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonfaith" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith">Jonfaith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2612144905" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11977143487338400883">I first read this about a hundred years ago. Someone gave a smart little leather bound copy and yesterday was an opportunity to place it in my jacket. I enjoyed such while my wife was inside at a hair appointment.<br/><br/>The story regards the plight of a clerk, an Everyman, a copyist by trade and largely oblivious to the world around him. He has no grasp of social mechanics and lives modestly without vice or hobby. It is to his horror that he discovers that his overcoat is disintegrating. He goes to a t</span>
  <span id="freeText11977143487338400883" style="display:none">I first read this about a hundred years ago. Someone gave a smart little leather bound copy and yesterday was an opportunity to place it in my jacket. I enjoyed such while my wife was inside at a hair appointment.<br /><br />The story regards the plight of a clerk, an Everyman, a copyist by trade and largely oblivious to the world around him. He has no grasp of social mechanics and lives modestly without vice or hobby. It is to his horror that he discovers that his overcoat is disintegrating. He goes to a tailor, who is himself an amazing literary construction. Our clerk must economize and saves for a new coat. Upon completion it is a sartorial marvel and suddenly our Everyman has an unexpected status from his peers, but not the skills to navigate such. He attends a party and loses himself in champagne. What follows is simple street crime. Gogol then displays the absence of recourse or justice in the contemporary bureaucracy. Matters go a bit supernatural and the reader can only marvel at the deft skill displayed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11977143487338400883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12226125356507014054" style="display:none">To be able to criticize a system by only explaining the interaction of people with each other!..<br />I wonder what Gogol would feel if he found out that nothing had changed after 200 years and that all the class differences had deepened. .<br /><br />Heavy as well as light, dramatic as well as comical, realistic as well as surrealistic, naturalistic and metaphysical, it is impossible to put a label on this narrative.<br />But the possible emotional fluctuations of the reader -me- unmistakably and instinctively mean that I have just read something great. yet, timeless.<br /><br />It would be extremely difficult for me to answer the question whether this work was ultimately a comedy, a parody, or indeed a tragedy.. but it surly has consolidated the idea that life is a BITCH then you just die!.</span>

<div id="review_1247489343" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1247489343" />
    <a title="Gautam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32002534-gautam"><img alt="Gautam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572713983p2/32002534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1247489343?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gautam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gautam" href="/user/show/32002534-gautam">Gautam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32002534-gautam?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32002534-gautam?shelf=russian-literature">russian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32002534-gautam?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1247489343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10569583366616691302">"We all come out from Gogol's 'overcoat'"- Dostoevesky <br/><br/>Gogol and Dostoevesky are singularly similar in their writings in many ways :<br/><br/>1. Dostoevesky was obsessed with gogol's writing in his youth and his novel 'The double' was considered to be a reply to Gogol's dead souls.<br/>2.The protagonist being impoverished and socially 'oppressed' and he leads the story. <br/>3. The 'Ambiguity' both create as if trying to leave the readers to fathom the conclusion or the essence (Our comprehensions may/may not be in</span>
  <span id="freeText10569583366616691302" style="display:none">"We all come out from Gogol's 'overcoat'"- Dostoevesky <br /><br />Gogol and Dostoevesky are singularly similar in their writings in many ways :<br /><br />1. Dostoevesky was obsessed with gogol's writing in his youth and his novel 'The double' was considered to be a reply to Gogol's dead souls.<br />2.The protagonist being impoverished and socially 'oppressed' and he leads the story. <br />3. The 'Ambiguity' both create as if trying to leave the readers to fathom the conclusion or the essence (Our comprehensions may/may not be intended by the writer).<br /><br />Gogol's overcoat is a caricature of 'life of an impoverished, socially oppressed man '(which was common in Russia). It tells us how impoverished people were despised and berated by the upper class, and how hard their lives were.The final part where Akaky's ghost hunts the upper class who had berated him, as I interpret it, is a form of retaliation to the social oppression and chasm between the classes . Akaky who was unable to react to the cruelty of the upper class succumbs to death,and later his oppressed and infurious soul seeks revenge for the pain and suffering which was inflicted upon him. <br /><br />4 stars on 5! <br /><br />-gautam<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10569583366616691302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('R/ayGtHXOLEiXYLram4/JSRTOoLhNS+GvVllLdK/kcs3dax4hZmxL++33YgSslSVc8Eh7AW7N8EIK3XZpBslRw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

And oh, what characters these are! Makar Dievushkin Alexievitch is a copy writer, barely squeaking by; Barbara Dobroselova Alexievna works as a seamstress, and both face the sort of everyday humiliation society puts upon the poor. These are people respected by no one, not even by themselves. These are folks too poor, in their circumstances, to marry; the love between them is a chaste and proper thing, a love that brings some readers to tears. But it isn\'t maudlin, either; Fyodor Dostoevsky has something profound to say about these people and this circumstance. And he says it very well. When the book was first published a leading Russian literary critic of the day -- Belinsky -- prophesied that Dostoevsky would become a literary giant. Chekhov was a Russian physician, dramatist and author who is considered to be one of the great<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10686358089415642223\" style=\"display:none\">*Illustrated*Includes Table of ContentsAnton Chekhov (1860-1904) may have suffered an untimely death, but he squeezed the most out of his 44 years of life. Chekhov was a Russian physician, dramatist and author who is considered to be one of the greatest writers in history, particularly in the genre of short stories. Chekhov’s major innovation near the end of the 19th century was in what became known as “stream-of-consciousness” writing, in which he eschewed common traditional story structure and simply wrote as though he was thinking aloud. Renowned writers like James Joyce and William Faulkner would eventually run with this theme, producing classics in the same vein. Chekhov was one of Russia’s most famous and popular writers in his time, producing well-received works like The Lady with the Dog and The Bishop. This edition of Chekhov’s short story, The Lady with the Dog, is specially formatted with a Table of Contents and is illustrated with over a dozen pictures of Chekhov.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/232932.Nikolai_Gogol">About Nikolai Gogol</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/232932.Nikolai_Gogol"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1303965602p3/232932.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/232932.Nikolai_Gogol">Nikolai Gogol
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,524
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15813157429201073112">RU:<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17684162.______________' title='Николай Гоголь' rel='nofollow noopener'>Николай Гоголь</a><br/><br/>Nikolai Gogol (born in Sorochyntsi, Poltava Governorate, Russian Empire, present-day Ukraine) - Russian writer of Ukrainian descent. <br/><br/>Gogol's mother was a descendant of Polish nobility. Gogol's father Vasyl Hohol-Yanovsky, a Ukrainian writer best known for his plays, died when Gogol was 15 years old. In 1820 Gogol went to a school of higher art in Nizhyn and remained there until 18</span>
  <span id="freeText15813157429201073112" style="display:none">RU:<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17684162.______________" title="Николай Гоголь" rel="nofollow noopener">Николай Гоголь</a><br /><br />Nikolai Gogol (born in Sorochyntsi, Poltava Governorate, Russian Empire, present-day Ukraine) - Russian writer of Ukrainian descent. <br /><br />Gogol's mother was a descendant of Polish nobility. Gogol's father Vasyl Hohol-Yanovsky, a Ukrainian writer best known for his plays, died when Gogol was 15 years old. In 1820 Gogol went to a school of higher art in Nizhyn and remained there until 1828. It was there that he began writing. Very early he developed a dark and secretive disposition, marked by a painful self-consciousness and boundless ambition. Equally early he developed an extraordinary talent for mimicry which later on made him a matchless reader of his own works.<br /><br />In 1828, on leaving school, Gogol came to Petersburg. He had hoped for literary fame and brought with him a Romantic poem of German idyllic life – Ganz Küchelgarten. He had it published, at his own expense, under the name of "V. Alov." The magazines he sent it to almost universally derided it. He bought all the copies and destroyed them, swearing never to write poetry again.<br /><br />Gogol was one of the first masters of the short story, alongside Alexander Pushkin, Prosper Mérimée, E. T. A. Hoffmann, and Nathaniel Hawthorne. He was in touch with the "literary aristocracy", and was taken up by Vasily Zhukovsky and Pyotr Pletnyov, and (in 1831) was introduced to Pushkin.<br /><br />In 1831, he brought out the first volume of his Ukrainian stories (Evenings on a Farm Near Dikanka), which met with immediate success. He followed it in 1832 with a second volume, and in 1835 by two volumes of stories entitled Mirgorod, as well as by two volumes of miscellaneous prose entitled Arabesques. At this time, Gogol developed a passion for Ukrainian history and tried to obtain an appointment to the history department at Kiev University. His fictional story Taras Bulba, based on the history of Ukrainian cossacks, was the result of this phase in his interests. <br /><br />Between 1832 and 1836 Gogol worked with great energy, though almost all his work has in one way or another its sources in his four years of contact with Pushkin. Only after the presentation, on 19 April 1836, of his comedy The Government Inspector (Revizor) that he finally came to believe in his literary vocation.<br /><br />From 1836 to 1848 he lived abroad, travelling throughout Germany and Switzerland, as well as spending the winter of 1836–1837 in Paris.<br /><br />Pushkin's death produced a strong impression on Gogol. His principal work during years following Pushkin's death was the satirical epic Dead Souls. Concurrently, he worked at other tasks – recast Taras Bulba and The Portrait, completed his second comedy, Marriage (Zhenitba), wrote the fragment Rome and his most famous short story, The Overcoat.<br /><br />After the triumph of Dead Souls, Gogol came to be regarded as a great satirist who lampooned the unseemly sides of Imperial Russia. However, Dead Souls was but the first part of a counterpart to The Divine Comedy. The first part represented the Inferno; the second part was to depict the gradual purification and transformation of the rogue Chichikov under the influence of virtuous publicans and governors – Purgatory.<br /><br />His last years were spent in restless movement throughout the country. He intensified his relationship with a church elder, Matvey Konstantinovsky. He seems to have strengthened in Gogol the fear of perdition by insisting on the sinfulness of all his imaginative work. His health was undermined by exaggerated ascetic practices and he fell into a state of deep depression. On the night of 24 February 1852, he burned some of his manuscripts, which contained most of the second part of Dead Souls. He explained this as a mistake, a practical joke played on him by the Devil. Soon thereafter, he took to bed, refused all food, and died in great pain nine days later. <br /><br />For goodread's profile in Russian, see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17684162._________________________" title="Николай Васильевич Гоголь" rel="nofollow noopener">Николай Васильевич Гоголь</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15813157429201073112" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/13183177">Trivia About The Overcoat</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
